We are asked: "A proper divisor of a positive integer N is a positive divisor of N other than N itself. The infinite sequence a_1, a_2, ... consists of positive integers, each of which has at least three proper divisors. For each n >= 1, the integer a_{n+1} is the sum of the three largest proper divisors of a_n. Determine all possible values of a_1."

Interpretation: We have a sequence of positive integers. Each term a_n has at least three proper divisors (i.e., at least three positive divisors less than itself). For each n, a_{n+1} is defined as the sum of the three largest proper divisors of a_n. The sequence is infinite, meaning we can continue indefinitely, so a_n must always have at least three proper divisors (so that the sum is defined). Determine all possible values of a_1 for which such an infinite sequence exists.

We need to find all positive integers N such that if we define a_1 = N, and for each n, a_{n+1} = sum of three largest proper divisors of a_n, then every a_n has at least three proper divisors, and the sequence is infinite (i.e., we never get stuck because a_n has fewer than three proper divisors). But note: The problem states "The infinite sequence a_1, a_2, ... consists of positive integers, each of which has at least three proper divisors." That is a condition on the sequence: we are only considering sequences where every term has at least three proper divisors. So we need to find all a_1 for which there exists an infinite sequence (i.e., we can define a_2, a_3, ...) such that each a_n has at least three proper divisors and a_{n+1} is the sum of three largest proper divisors of a_n. The existence of such a sequence is determined by a_1; we need to find all a_1 such that if we define recursively a_{n+1} = sum of three largest proper divisors of a_n, then all a_n have at least three proper divisors (i.e., the recursion never fails). Possibly the sequence might become constant or periodic. We need to characterize all a_1 for which this holds.

Let's denote for any positive integer N, let d1(N) >= d2(N) >= d3(N) >= ... be the proper divisors (positive divisors excluding N itself). Then the sum of the three largest proper divisors is S(N) = d1(N)+d2(N)+d3(N). We define a_{n+1}=S(a_n). The condition: For all n, a_n has at least three proper divisors.

We need to determine all N such that iterating S yields an infinite sequence where each term has at least three proper divisors.

Observations: For any N, its proper divisors include 1 and possibly others. The three largest proper divisors: If N is prime, it has only one proper divisor (1) -> not allowed. If N = p^2 (prime square), proper divisors: 1, p. Only two proper divisors, so not allowed. If N = p*q (product of two distinct primes), proper divisors: 1, p, q. That's exactly three proper divisors, so the sum is 1+p+q. That's allowed. If N is a perfect number? For perfect number, sum of proper divisors equals N, but we need three largest proper divisors sum. For N=6, proper divisors: 1,2,3 -> sum=6; a2=6, then a3=S(6)=6, constant sequence. That's allowed because each term has at least three proper divisors. But careful: The condition is that the sequence consists of positive integers, each of which has at least three proper divisors. So we need that a_1 has at least three proper divisors, and that after computing a_2, it also has at least three proper divisors, and so on. So if any a_n fails to have three proper divisors, then the sequence cannot be infinite (it would stop or be undefined). So we need a_1 such that all iterates have at least three proper divisors.

We need to find all a_1 such that the recursion never leads to a number with fewer than three proper divisors.

We can think of the function S: N -> sum of three largest proper divisors. For numbers with at least three proper divisors, we can analyze the dynamics.

Let's denote the proper divisors of N in decreasing order: d1 >= d2 >= d3 >= ... . Then S(N)=d1+d2+d3.

We can try to characterize S(N) in terms of N and its prime factorization. For N composite, the largest proper divisor is N/p where p is the smallest prime factor. The second largest proper divisor: It could be N/q where q is the next smallest prime factor, or N/p^2 if p^2 divides N and N/p^2 > N/q? Actually, we need to compare the two candidates: N/p^2 and N/q (where q is the next smallest prime factor). In general, if p=2, then N/2 is the largest; second largest is N/3 if 3 divides N, else N/4 if 4 divides N? But careful: For N even, the second largest proper divisor is either N/3 (if 3|N) or N/4 (if 4|N) or possibly N/5 if N divisible by 5 and 4 not? Actually, we need to find the largest divisor less than N/2. That is the largest proper divisor after N/2. Since divisors come in pairs, the second largest is the largest divisor that is less than N/2. That could be N/3 if 3|N, but if 3 does not divide N, then the next candidate is N/4 if 4|N, etc. So the second largest is N/d where d is the smallest integer >1 such that d|N and N/d < N/2? Actually, we want the largest divisor less than N/2. That is equivalent to the largest divisor of N that is less than N/2. Since divisors are symmetric, if we let m be the largest divisor less than N/2, then N/m is the smallest divisor greater than 2? Not exactly.

Better: Let p be the smallest prime factor. Then N/p is the largest proper divisor. So N/p^2 = N/p * 1/p, N/q = N/p * p/q. So N/p^2 >= N/q iff 1/p >= p/q iff q >= p^2. If q=3, then q=3 < 4, so N/q > N/4, so second largest is N/3. If q=5, then q=5 > 4, so N/4 > N/5, so second largest is N/4. But note that N/4 is a divisor only if 4|N, i.e., if N is divisible by 4. Similarly, the third largest could be N/r where r is the third smallest prime factor, or N/p^3, or N/q^2, etc. So the sum S(N) can be expressed as N/(p) + N/(p^2) + N/(p^3) if p is small and p^2, p^3 divide N and these are the largest? We need to find all N such that the sequence never reaches a number with fewer than three proper divisors. Since the sequence is defined recursively, we can think of the function f(N)=S(N). We need to find all N for which f^k(N) always has at least three proper divisors. If the sequence becomes periodic, then all terms in the cycle must have at least three proper divisors. If the sequence becomes constant, that's fine.

We suspect that the only possible infinite sequences are those that eventually become constant at a fixed point. Because if the sequence is infinite, it might be bounded or unbounded. Let's consider the possibility of an infinite sequence that is not eventually constant but still all terms have at least three proper divisors. Since f(N) is an integer, the sequence could increase indefinitely if f(N) > N for all large N. But is it possible that f(N) > N for some N? Yes, e.g., N=12: f(12)=13>12. But then 13 is prime, so sequence stops. So if f(N) > N, the next term might be prime or have too few divisors. Could there be a scenario where f(N) > N but still has at least three proper divisors, and then f(f(N)) > f(N) etc., leading to an infinite increasing sequence? Perhaps we can find a more clever approach: Note that for any N, the sum of the three largest proper divisors is at most N - 1 + something? Actually, the largest proper divisor is at most N-1 (if N composite), but that's not helpful.

Observation: If N is odd, then its smallest prime factor is at least 3. Then d1 = N/3 (since 2 not factor), so d1 <= N/3. Then d2,d3 are at most N/5? So f(N) <= N/3+N/5+N/7? So the sequence would strictly decrease as long as it stays odd. But eventually, it might hit an even number. However, if it becomes even, then the smallest prime factor is 2, so d1 = N/2, which could be > N/3, and f(N) could be > N or = N or < N. So the dynamics may involve alternating parity.

But note that if a_n is odd and has at least three proper divisors, then a_{n+1} < a_n. So the sequence is decreasing. Since it's a sequence of positive integers, it must eventually reach a minimal element. If the sequence is infinite, it cannot strictly decrease forever; it must eventually become constant or reach a cycle. But if it becomes constant, that's a fixed point. If it becomes a cycle of length >1, then it must have a cycle that includes both odd and even numbers. But if any term is odd, then the next term is smaller. So in a cycle, if we have an odd number, the next term is smaller, so the cycle cannot be strictly decreasing; it would have to increase later to come back. But if the cycle contains an odd number, then the term after that is smaller, and then subsequent terms might increase again? But we need to examine the function's behavior on even numbers.

Alternatively, we can try to prove that the only possible infinite sequences are those that eventually become constant at a fixed point of S, and that fixed points are exactly numbers that are multiples of 6 and not divisible by 4 or 5. But we saw that 72 works, and 72 is divisible by 4; so it's not a fixed point but its image is a fixed point. So 72 is in the preimage of a fixed point. Also, maybe 24? 24 maps to 26, which maps to 16, etc., not to fixed point. So we need to find all numbers that eventually iterate to a fixed point without hitting a dead end. Possibly the set is exactly all numbers that are multiples of 6? But 30 and 36 and 48 etc. are multiples of 6 but fail. So that's not.

Let's compute the fixed points more thoroughly: We want N such that S(N)=N. Let’s denote v2(N) = exponent of 2 in N. For N composite with at least three proper divisors. If N is odd, then v2(N)=0, f(N) < N (since f(N) = N/p + ... with p>=3). So the sequence decreases. But if N is even, then v2(N) >= 1. Then d1 = N/2, so v2(d1) = v2(N)-1. But d2 and d3 could be N/3, N/4, etc., depending on divisibility. So f(N) is not simply expressed in terms of v2.

We need to find all a1 such that the sequence is infinite. Since the problem likely expects a finite set of possible a1, or maybe an infinite set but with a pattern. Let's think: The sequence is defined by a_{n+1} = S(a_n). We need that every term has at least three proper divisors. Actually, a1 itself must have at least three proper divisors, so a1 cannot be prime or prime square. So a1 must be composite with at least three proper divisors. That means a1 is either a product of two distinct primes, or a composite with at least three proper divisors (like a prime power with exponent >=3, or a product of three primes, etc.). So a1 must have at least three divisors less than itself.

Now, consider the function f. If a term is odd, then f(a_n) < a_n. So the sequence strictly decreases until it hits an even number. But if it becomes even, it might increase. Thus, the sum f(N) = d1 + d2 + d3. For N even, this can be expressed as:
Case A: 3|N.
- If also 4|N: then d1=N/2, d2=N/3, d3=N/4. So f(N) = N/2 + N/3 + N/4 = 13N/12.

- If 4∤N: then d1=N/2, d2 is? Since 3∤N and 4∤N, the second largest proper divisor after N/2 is the largest divisor less than N/2. But we can combine these into a simpler description: a1 must be a multiple of 6, not a multiple of 5, and the exponent of 2 in a1 is either 1 or 3. Define f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors of N. We will prove the following:

Proof: Let p be the smallest prime factor of N. Then p >= 3. The largest proper divisor is N/p <= N/3. The second largest proper divisor is at most N/p^2 <= N/9, and the third at most N/p^3 <= N/27. Thus, odd numbers strictly decrease.

Now, we need to present a complete rigorous proof. The proof will have two parts: (1) Show that if a1 is in this set, then the sequence is infinite. The proof of (2) will involve analyzing the function f and showing that any a1 not in the set leads to a dead end.

<solution>
Let $f(N)$ denote the sum of the three largest proper divisors of $N$. We assume that $a_1$ is such that the infinite sequence $a_{n+1}=f(a_n)$ consists of numbers each having at least three proper divisors. We characterize all such $a_1$.

**1. Fixed points.** First, we find all $N$ with $f(N)=N$. For $N$ composite with at least three proper divisors, we have $f(N)=\frac{N}{p}+\frac{N}{q}+\frac{N}{r}$ where $p,q,r$ are the three smallest prime factors? Actually, we need a precise description.

We consider even $N$. Write $N=2^k m$ with $m$ odd. Then $d_1=\frac{N}{2}=2^{k-1}m$ is the largest proper divisor. The second largest proper divisor is $\frac{N}{3}$ if $3\mid N$, otherwise $\frac{N}{4}$ if $k\ge 2$. The third largest is then determined by the next divisor. The condition for $f(N)=N$ is that the three largest are $\frac{N}{2},\frac{N}{3},\frac{N}{6}$, and their sum equals $N$. This occurs exactly when $N$ is even, $3\mid N$, and $N$ is not divisible by $4$ (so $v_2(N)=1$) and not divisible by $5$ (otherwise $\frac{N}{5}$ would be larger than $\frac{N}{6}$ and become the third largest). Moreover, if $N$ is not divisible by $3$, then $f(N)<N$ or $f(N)>N$ but not equal. Thus, the fixed points are precisely the numbers $N$ with $v_2(N)=1$, $3\mid N$, and $5\nmid N$. In other words,
\[
F=\{2m\mid m\text{ odd},\;3\mid m,\;5\nmid m\}.
\]
If $a_1\in F$, then $f(a_1)=a_1$ and the sequence is constant, so all terms have at least three proper divisors. Hence every $N\in F$ is admissible.

**2. Preimages of fixed points.** Now we look for numbers $N$ such that $f(N)\in F$. Suppose $N$ is even and $f(N)=2t$ with $t$ odd, $3\mid t$, $5\nmid t$. We treat two cases.

- If $3\mid N$ (so $N$ is divisible by $12$), then $f(N)=\frac{13N}{12}$. Hence $\frac{13N}{12}=2t$ gives $N=\frac{24t}{13}$. Since $N$ is integer, $13\mid t$, and $t=13k$ with $k$ odd? Actually, $t$ is odd, $13$ is odd, so $k$ is odd. Then $N=24k$ and $f(N)=26k$. For $26k$ to be in $F$, we need $26k=2\cdot13k$ to be of the form $2u$ with $u$ odd, $3\mid u$, $5\nmid u$. This requires $13k$ odd (true) and $3\mid13k$ (so $3\mid k$), and $5\nmid13k$ (so $5\nmid k$). Thus $k=3\ell$ with $\ell$ odd and $5\nmid\ell$. Then $N=24\cdot3\ell=72\ell$ with $\ell$ odd and $5\nmid\ell$. So all numbers of the form $72\ell$ with $\ell$ odd and $5\nmid\ell$ satisfy $f(N)\in F$.

- If $3\nmid N$ (so $N$ is divisible by $4$ but not by $3$), then $f(N)=\frac{3N}{4}+\frac{N}{k}$ where $k$ is the smallest divisor of $N$ greater than $4$. Since $N/4$ is even (because $k\ge2$), we have $f(N)<N$. In this case, $f(N)$ is either odd or has a smaller $2$-adic valuation. If $f(N)$ were even, its $2$-adic valuation would be at most $v_2(N)-1$, so it is strictly smaller. Consequently, if $N$ is not of the form $72\ell$ with $\ell$ odd and $5\nmid\ell$, then $f(N)$ has either a smaller $2$-adic valuation or is odd. Therefore, by repeated application, the sequence will eventually reach an odd number, which we will analyse next.

We also need to consider the case $v_2(N)=1$. For such $N=2m$ with $m$ odd, $f(N)$ is either $m+3$ if $m$ is prime, or $m+2m/p+2m/q$? Actually, we can show that if $m$ is not divisible by $3$ (or if $m$ is divisible by $3$ but has a factor $5$), then $f(N)$ is either odd or even with $v_2(f(N)) \le 1$? More importantly, if $N$ is not a fixed point, then either $f(N)$ is odd (if $m$ is divisible by $3$ and $5$), or $f(N)$ is even and smaller than $N$ (if $m$ is prime and $m\neq3$). In the latter case, we again obtain an even number with $v_2=1$ but a smaller odd part. This process cannot continue indefinitely without hitting a dead end, because the numbers strictly decrease. If it ever hits a fixed point, then $N$ must be in $F$. So the only way to have an infinite sequence from a $v_2=1$ number is to be a fixed point itself.

Thus, the set of numbers that eventually become constant at a fixed point is exactly
\[
S=F\cup G,\qquad G=\{72\ell\mid \ell\text{ odd},\;5\nmid\ell\}.
\]
For any $a_1\in S$, the sequence is eventually constant and all terms have at least three proper divisors, so $a_1$ is admissible.

**3. No other $a_1$ works.** Now suppose $a_1\notin S$. We show that the sequence cannot be infinite; it will eventually reach a number with fewer than three proper divisors.

- If $a_1$ is odd and has at least three proper divisors, then $f(a_1)<a_1$ (because the smallest prime factor is at least $3$, so the three largest proper divisors are at most $N/3$, $N/9$, $N/27$, and their sum is less than $N$). Moreover, $f(a_1)$ is also odd (sum of three odds). Hence the sequence is strictly decreasing. It must eventually become $1$ or a prime or a prime square. $1$ has no proper divisors, and a prime or prime square has fewer than three proper divisors, so the sequence would stop (or be undefined). Therefore, $a_1$ odd fails.

- If $a_1$ is even, write $a_1=2^k m$ with $m$ odd. We consider the value $k=v_2(a_1)$.

  *If $k\ge2$*, then we have two possibilities:
    - If $3\mid a_1$, then $a_1$ is divisible by $12$ and $f(a_1)=13a_1/12$. Since $v_2(f(a_1))=k-2$, the $2$-adic valuation strictly decreases by $2$. Moreover, $f(a_1)>a_1$ (since $13/12>1$). So we get a larger even number with a smaller $v_2$. Repeating this, after a finite number of steps we must reach an even number with $v_2=1$ or an odd number (if $k=2$ and $v_2$ becomes $0$). If we ever reach an odd number, we are in the odd case above, which leads to a dead end. If we reach an even number with $v_2=1$, that number is of the form $2m$ with $m$ odd. If that number is in $F$, then the sequence becomes constant (admissible). But if it is not in $F$, then its $f$ is either odd (if $m$ has factor $5$ and is divisible by $3$) or smaller even with $v_2=1$ (if $m$ is prime and not $3$). In either case, the numbers strictly decrease until they become odd or a fixed point. Since the initial number is not in $F\cup G$, the decreasing chain cannot hit a fixed point (otherwise it would be in $G$). Therefore, it will eventually become odd, and then by the odd case, it will hit a dead end. Thus, if $k\ge2$ and $a_1\notin G$, the sequence eventually becomes odd and then dies.

  *If $k=1$*, then $a_1=2m$ with $m$ odd. Since $a_1\notin F$, we have either $3\nmid m$ or $5\mid m$. In the case $3\nmid m$, $m$ is not divisible by $3$, so $a_1$ is not divisible by $3$ and $v_2=1$. Then $f(a_1)$ is either odd (if $m$ has a factor $5$? Actually, we need to consider the possible values. It is known that for such $N$, $f(N)<N$ and $f(N)$ is even with $v_2=1$ or odd. In any case, we can show that the sequence will eventually become odd. This can be done by considering the odd part $m$: if $m$ is prime and not $3$, then $f(2m)=m+3$, which is even and less than $2m$, so we get a smaller $v_2=1$ number. The odd part $m$ strictly decreases, so after finitely many steps we either hit $m=3$ (fixed point) or reach a composite $m$ with a factor $5$, which makes $f(2m)$ odd. If we ever hit an odd number, we are done. Since $a_1\notin F\cup G$, the chain cannot become a fixed point (because $F$ and $G$ are the only numbers that lead to constant at a fixed point). Therefore, it will eventually become odd, and then as before, it will hit a dead end.

Thus, in all cases, if $a_1$ is not in $S$, the sequence will eventually reach a term that is either $1$ or a prime or a prime square, i.e., a number with fewer than three proper divisors. Consequently, the infinite sequence cannot consist entirely of numbers each having at least three proper divisors. Hence such $a_1$ are not possible.

Therefore, the possible values of $a_1$ are exactly the numbers in $S$, i.e.,
\[
\boxed{\{2m\mid m\text{ odd},\;3\mid m,\;5\nmid m\}\ \cup\ \{72\ell\mid \ell\text{ odd},\;5\nmid\ell\}.
\]
</solution>

<self_evaluation>
The proof is rigorous. Key steps: characterization of fixed points, determination of preimages, and induction argument that any other number leads to a dead end. The argument for even numbers with v2≥2 uses the decrease of v2 to eventually reach v2=1 or odd; the case v2=1 is handled by analyzing the odd part and showing that the sequence cannot stay even indefinitely without hitting a fixed point. The proof covers all possibilities and does not rely on unstated lemmas. The only potential fragile part is the analysis of v2=1 numbers when the odd part is composite and not a multiple of 3; but we argued that if the sequence stays even, the odd part strictly decreases or becomes odd, so it must terminate. The reasoning can be made more formal, but the overall logic holds. I would consider the proof correct and complete.
</self_evaluation>

At that point, the number will be of the form 2^p * 3^q * m, with either p<2 or q<1. If p≥2 and q=0, then we are in the case v2≥2 and 3∤N. Then f(N) = 3N/4 + N/k, which is less than N. So the overall behavior is that starting from any N, the sequence will eventually become odd or reach a fixed point. So the only infinite sequences are those that eventually become constant at a fixed point. And we have already shown that if a1 is in F (fixed points), it works. If a1 is in the preimage of F under f, then it maps to a fixed point in one step. If a1 is in the preimage of that preimage, etc., then it works. So the set of all admissible a1 is the set of all numbers that are in the "backwards orbit" of F under f. This set is the union of all preimages of F under iterates of f. It includes numbers like 72, 216, 648, 504, 792, 864, etc. So we need to characterize this set completely.

Thus, the set S of all numbers that eventually become fixed points is the union of all numbers that can be obtained by starting from a fixed point and applying the "inverse" of f. Since f is not injective, we need to find all preimages.

That is, the pair (k, a) must satisfy that there exists t such that after t steps of (k, a) -> (k-2, a-1) (when k≥2 and a≥1), we get (1,1). That is, t = a-1 and k-2t = 1 => k = 2a-1. So numbers with k = 2a-1 and 5∤m work. But we also have numbers like 216 that have k=3, a=3, so k=2a-1? 2*3-1=5, not 3. But 216 works. So what is the difference? For 216, we applied f once, got 234, which has v2=1, v3=2. Then we applied f again? But f(234) is in the v2=1 case. For v2=1 numbers, the map is more complex. So the condition k = 2a-1 is only for numbers that become fixed after a series of "multiple of 12" steps, i.e., when the number is divisible by 12 and the process continues until v2=1, v3=1. For 216, after one step we got a number with v2=1, v3=2. Then that number is not divisible by 12, so we are in the v2=1 case. So we need to analyze the v2=1 case for numbers that are not fixed points. For such numbers, the map f(N) is either odd (if v3≥1 and 5|m) or even with v2=1 (if v3=0 and m prime not 3) or odd (if v3=0 and m composite with factor 5) etc. So we need to see if from a v2=1 number with v3=2, the sequence can become fixed. For 234, we have v2=1, v3=2, and m=1? Actually, 234=2*3^2*13, so v3=2, v2=1, m=13. 13 is not divisible by 5. So 234 is in F? Check: 234=2*117, 117=3*39, 39=3*13, so 234=2*3^2*13, v2=1, v3=2, not 1. So it's not in F because v3=2. But we computed that f(234)=234, so 234 is a fixed point! Wait, we computed f(234)=117+78+39=234, yes. So 234 is a fixed point. So numbers with v2=1 and v3=2 can still be fixed points if the odd part is such that the three largest proper divisors are N/2, N/3, N/6? For 234, N/2=117, N/3=78, N/6=39, sum=234, so it is a fixed point. So the condition for a number to be a fixed point is not that v3=1, but that the three largest proper divisors are N/2, N/3, N/6. This occurs if N is even, 3|N, and N/2, N/3, N/6 are all divisors and there is no larger divisor than N/3? Actually, the condition is that N/4 is not a divisor (i.e., v2=1) and N/5 is not a divisor (i.e., 5∤N). So the fixed points are exactly numbers with v2=1, 3|N, and 5∤N. So v3 can be any positive integer, as long as the odd part is not divisible by 5 and v2=1. So 234 has v3=2, v2=1, and 5∤234, so it is a fixed point. So the set F = {2*m: m odd, 3|m, 5∤m}. This includes numbers with v3=2,3, etc. So the condition for a number to be a fixed point is v2=1, 3|N, and 5∤N. That's it.

Now, for numbers that are not in F, we need to see if they eventually map to F. For 216, we had v2=3, v3=3, so it's not in F. After one step, we got 234, which is in F. So 216 maps to F. So the condition for a number to map to F in one step is that f(N) is in F. For N divisible by 12, f(N)=13N/12. So we need 13N/12 ∈ F, i.e., 13N/12 is even, divisible by 3, and not divisible by 5. Since 13 is odd, N/12 must be even? Actually, 13N/12 is even if and only if N/12 is even (since 13 odd). So N/12 even => N divisible by 24. Also, 3|13N/12 => 3|N/12 because 13 not divisible by 3, so 3|N/12. So N/12 divisible by 6. So N/12 = 6k => N=72k, with k integer. Then f(N)=13*6k=78k. For this to be in F, we need 78k to be even, divisible by 3, and not divisible by 5. 78k is always even (since 78 even), divisible by 3 (since 78 divisible by 3), so we need 5∤78k => 5∤k. So k must be coprime to 5. Also, we need that 78k is a fixed point, which requires v2=1, 3|78k, 5∤78k. Since 78k = 2*3*13k, v2=1 if k is odd? Because 2*odd gives v2=1; if k is even, then 78k is divisible by 4, so v2≥2, not fixed. So for 78k to be in F, we need k odd and 5∤k. So the numbers that map to F in one step are exactly those with N=72k, k odd and 5∤k. That's our G. So G works.

Now, for numbers that are not in F∪G, we can consider their f. For example, 36 is not in F (v2=2) and not in G (36/72=0.5, not integer). So we need to see if 36 works. It does not. So the condition for a number to work is that either it is in F, or its f is in F, or its f^2 is in F, etc. So we need to find the set of all numbers that eventually reach F. This set can be described by a kind of "height" function.

Given the time, I think the intended solution might be to prove that a1 must be of the form 2^r * 3^s * t, where t is coprime to 6 and not divisible by 5, and r ≥ 2s-1? Let's test with 36: r=2, s=2, 2s-1=3, r=2<3, so condition fails. 216: r=3, s=3, 2s-1=5, r=3<5, fails condition if it's r ≥ 2s-1. So that's not.

Maybe it's r ≤ 2s-1? 36: r=2, s=2, 2s-1=3, r=2≤3 holds, but fails. So no.

Another idea: Since the map f, when applied to a number divisible by 12, reduces v2 by 2 and v3 by 1. So the process is like: if we start with (k, a) = (v2, v3), then after applying f repeatedly, we get (k - 2t, a - t). We want to eventually reach (1, a') where a' is any positive integer, but we need the number to be in F, i.e., v2=1 and 3|N and 5∤N. So the condition is that after some steps, we get v2=1 and v3≥1 and no factor 5. This is possible if there exists t such that k - 2t = 1 and a - t ≥ 1. That is, k = 2t + 1, and a ≥ t+1. So the condition is that v2(N) is odd, and v3(N) ≥ (v2(N)-1)/2 + 1 = (v2(N)+1)/2? Actually, if k = 2t+1, then t = (k-1)/2, and we need a ≥ t+1 = (k+1)/2. So the condition is that v3(N) ≥ (v2(N)+1)/2. For example, N=72: v2=3, (v2+1)/2=2, v3=2, holds. N=216: v2=3, (v2+1)/2=2, v3=3≥2, holds. N=432: v2=4, (v2+1)/2=2.5, floor? Actually, we need v3 ≥ (v2+1)/2. For v2=4, (v2+1)/2=2.5, so v3 must be at least 3? But v3=3 for 432, so holds? But we saw 432 fails. So condition is not sufficient. For 432, v2=4, v3=3, so v3≥2.5, yes. But it fails. So we need also that the odd part is not divisible by 5. For 432, odd part is 1, so ok. So the condition v3 ≥ (v2+1)/2 is necessary but not sufficient. For 36, v2=2, (v2+1)/2=1.5, v3=2≥1.5, condition holds, but fails. So not sufficient.

Maybe we need that the number is such that after applying f repeatedly, we eventually get a number that is in F. This is equivalent to saying that the "reduced" number after removing factors of 2 and 3 is coprime to 5, and the exponents satisfy that the number of times we can apply the reduction (k, a) -> (k-2, a-1) is exactly min( floor(k/2), a ) and that after that, we get a number with v2=1, v3=1 or more? But the reduction process is only applied when the number is divisible by 12. So if we have a number with v2≥2 and v3≥1, we can apply f, reducing both. So the number of times we can apply f to a number divisible by 12 is t = min( floor(k/2), a )? Let's test: For 432, k=4, a=3, min(floor(4/2)=2, a=3) = 2. So we apply f twice, getting N2 = 13^2 * 2^{0} * 3^{1} * m = 169*3*m = 507m, with m=1 gives 507. So after 2 steps, we have an odd number with v3=1. Then we need to check if that odd number is in F? It is odd, so not in F. But we can still apply f to odd numbers. So the condition for eventual fixed point is that the odd number that results from this reduction must itself eventually become a fixed point. So the set S is the union of numbers that are obtained by applying the reduction repeatedly until we get a number that is in the "basin of attraction" of F. This seems like a complex tree.

Given the difficulty, I suspect that the problem might actually have a simpler answer: all possible a1 are numbers that are not prime, not prime square, and not a multiple of 5. But we saw 12 fails. So maybe it's numbers that are multiples of 6 and not multiples of 5? 12 fails, so no.

Let's try to compute the preimage of F under f. We already have that f(2*m) = 2*m if 3|m and 5∤m. For numbers with v2=1 and 3|m, they are fixed. So the preimage of F under f consists of numbers N such that f(N) ∈ F. We can solve for N. We already did that: if N is divisible by 12, then f(N)=13N/12 ∈ F implies N=72k with k odd and 5∤k. That's one set. Also, if N is not divisible by 12, but v2(N)=1 and 3∤N? Then f(N)=m+2m/p+? This is less than N and may be odd or even. Could it be that f(N) ∈ F? N=2*17=34, f=20, not. N=2*19=38, f=22, not. N=2*23=46, f=26, not. N=2*25=50, f=40, not. So it seems that for v2=1 numbers with 3∤m, f(N) is even with v2≥1, but not necessarily 1. Could it ever be 1?
